The two supreme audit institutions have set the following principles for comparing bridge prices: start from the actual price paid for the bridge excluding value added tax (VAT rates differ in the Czech Republic and the SNR and are subject to changes); do not take into account prices for special bridge foundations; convert prices in CZK into € according to the rate of the European Central Bank valid as of 30. 12. 2005 (CZK 29 = € 1).
On both D 8 and A 17 there are bridges of various constructions (steel-concrete, prestressed reinforced concrete, coupled steel-concrete and steel) and different sizes.
In order to ensure the homogeneity of the examined structures, the bridges were divided into groups according to the type of construction.
Within these groups, the size of the bridge was also taken into account in the analyses.
By far the most common type of construction were concrete bridges on both sides of the border (20 bridges on D 8, 36 bridges on A 17).
The analysis focused mainly on this group of bridges, as the abundance of the set allowed the use of mathematical-statistical methods with a high degree of reliability of the results.
The inspections revealed that the prices per m 2 of concrete bridges on both D 8 and A 17 depend on the size of the bridge (its length, area): the larger the bridge, the lower the price per m2 of the bridge structure.
For German bridges, the dependence of price on size was stronger than for Czech bridges (see graphs below).
Two concrete bridges on the A 17 and four on the D 8 have been identified, the price of which is significantly higher.
To find these bridges, a method was used to detect atypical values in the set of numbers, as well as a regression analysis taking into account the dependence of the price of the bridge on its size (the distance from the regression curve upwards is decisive for determining atypically expensive bridges).
However, information on the atypically high price of those bridges should only be taken as an indication of which bridges should be targeted.
In order to draw a generally applicable conclusion, the reasons for the high price in each identified case should be analysed.
One such cause may be inefficient administrative measures, or environmental requirements or any other plausible reason.
In view of the fact that Czech concrete bridges are on average longer than German ones (see the table below), the proven negative dependence of the bridge price on its length could lead to the assumption that Czech bridges should be cheaper.
The hypothesis of lower prices of Czech concrete bridges is also supported by lower labour costs in the construction industry in the Czech Republic compared to Germany.
On the contrary, the results of the analysis show that the prices of concrete bridges on the D 8 are higher than on the A 17.
During the joint meetings, two possible causes of this were identified:
1st
In Germany, most bridges are awarded as separate contracts.
Such an award allows small and medium-sized regional firms to participate in the tender.
In the Czech Republic, many bridges are awarded as part of a contract for a part of the motorway route that is several kilometres long.
Small and medium-sized companies do not participate in such a competition because the contract is too large for them.
2nd
In the Czech Republic there is greater variability in bridge construction than in Germany.
In this context, the SAO found that the type of bridge construction is decided long before its construction.
The decision is taken at the time when the RMD submits the planning application.
Although the RMD selects bridge types from several variants at this time, economy plays a minor role in decision-making.
A general conclusion on the prices of bridges in the Czech Republic and Germany could only be reached on the basis of a thorough analysis of the construction work market in both countries, which would, however, go beyond the scope of this auditing operation.
The border bridge is the most expensive coupled steel-concrete bridge on the D8 and A17.
In the case of tunnels, the prices of tunnels on D 8 were found to be twice as high as those in Germany.
However, a comparison of the main construction-technical parameters of the tunnels on the D 8 with those in Germany did not show any significant difference.
Significant price differences could be attributed to the fact that there is no real competition in the field of tunnel construction in the Czech Republic.
However, the small number of tunnels did not allow for generally applicable conclusions.
Effectiveness and efficiency of the construction of the Dresden motorway - Prague
The independent Czech Republic was established in 1993.
In the same year, the government approved a concept for the development of the motorway network.
The D8 motorway was to be gradually extended from Prague to the state border with Germany.
The section No 0805 through České středohoří should have been acquired earlier than the border section No 0807.
The Czech Republic is procuring the D8 motorway as part of the programme financing.
The programme No. 327 220 - Construction of the D8 motorway Prague - Ústí nad Labem - state border of the Czech Republic/Germany - set two objectives for the construction of the D8 motorway:
1st
improve the connection between Prague and Ústí nad Labem - a national objective;
2nd
improve connections between Prague and Berlin in the IV Trans-European Transport Corridor Berlin - Istanbul - international destination.
According to the programme, the construction of the section leading through České středohoří was to be completed in June 2008 and the construction of the border section no. 0807 in June 2007.
It should be added here that the completion date is not the same as the date of commissioning.
The SAO found that the preparation of the construction of the section leading through České středohoří is now in the stage of an official zoning decision.
Environmental activists have challenged the decision with lawsuits.
The actions do not have the force of res judicata.
It cannot therefore be excluded that the section leading through the České středohoří will be completed several years later than stated in the programme documentation.
In the summer of 2006, the Czech Ministry of Transport proposed postponing the completion date of section 0805 to 2010.
As a result of these facts, the construction of the border section did not achieve the national objective of improving the connection between Prague and Ústí nad Labem.
The RMD carried out an estimation of capital expenditures for the border section D 8 No. 0807 and the section No. 0805 through České středohoří in 1999-2001.
In these capital expenditures, the investment costs included are estimated at about €639 million combined for both sections 0805 and 0807.

The economic analysis carried out in 2001 was based on the assumption that the border section and the section through České středohoří would be completed in time.
At the time of the inspection, however, it is clear that the section through České středohoří will not be opened on the original date.
This will reduce the above EIRR values.
The cost of both sections excluding VAT, calculated at 31. 12. 2005, were €897 million, i.e. 27.4% higher than the cost level used in the 2001 economic analysis.
However, there is a risk that section 0805 will be run on a different, more costly route, which would affect the above-mentioned threshold of economic viability.
The economic analysis showed that the construction of both sections of the motorway is economically viable with a traffic intensity of at least 18 000 vehicles per day in 2010.
The forecast for 2008 was 22,940 vehicles, but the study dealing with the issue of bypass routes for the missing section through České středohoří calculates the traffic intensity of 12,600 to 13,750 vehicles per day, which pushes the investment below the threshold of economic viability.
With a higher load on bypass routes for the missing section through České středohoří, there is a high probability of congestion and thus an increase in external costs, which were not taken into account in the original economic analysis.
The above-mentioned reduction in revenues and cost increases mean that the already low EIRR expected to be achieved by the construction of the D 8 frontier section will not be achieved.
The construction of the D 8 border section is therefore inefficient.
The construction of the A 17 also involved similar cost overruns as those two sections on the D 8.
As part of the overall economic assessment of the Federal Road Development Plan for long-distance transport for 1992, the Federal Ministry of Transport of Germany envisaged costs of about €320 million.
The resulting cost-benefit ratio was 4.6.
In the meantime, however, costs have soared to about €670 million, reducing the cost-benefit ratio by more than half.
In addition to the parallel audit carried out jointly with the SAO, BRH audited the preparation and construction of the A 17 motorway in 2003 and 2005.
The BRH found that the competent State approval authority had requested an off-road tunnel for Section A 17, which was not needed.
During the inspection, the BRH managed to convince the Federal Ministry and the German Road Construction Administration that the tunnel was not necessary.
This resulted in savings of around €10 million in construction costs alone and €0.15 million in annual operating costs.
During the assessment of the first section A 17, the BRH found that the construction project had not been properly prepared.
The following example illustrates this:
At the preparatory stage, the Road Construction Administration did not carry out a survey of the conditions of the bedrock in the Dölzschen rocks because the site was difficult to access.
This was done only after the tunnel cutting machine hit the rock.
Tests showed that the rocks inside were more weathered than anticipated during the tender.
As a result, costly measures such as anchoring, grouting and stone-falling safety nets had to be taken, costing about €3.3 million.
It's not going to be e
Expenditure of this kind includes costs related to the interruption of construction operations (€3.2 million), further training of drilling staff (€0.5 million) and rising wages and expenditure on goods due to the overrun of the project duration (€7 million).
As a result, the cost overrun amounted to around €10.7 million, which was not matched by any added value.
Based on the lessons learned from this and other audited projects, the BRH asked the Federal Ministry to apply stricter leadership in its supervision of road construction works.
In doing so, the Federal Ministry should give the Roads and Motorways Directorates the necessary impetus to implement quality assurance systems.
Benefits of cooperation for BRH and SAO Cooperation fulfilled the intention of both audit institutions.
The BRH obtained audit findings on the preparation, assignment and course of the construction of the border bridge.
Both SAIs have built a database of bridge and tunnel prices on which future inspections can rely.
The SAO found that the main construction-technical parameters of bridges, tunnels and the D8 motorway did not differ from those of A17.
Auditors of both institutions participating in the cooperation gained new experience which they can use during further audits.

Coordinated control of funds earmarked for financing projects implemented under the Operational Programme Cross-Border Cooperation Czech Republic - Republic of Poland 2007 - 2013
INTRODUCTION
The developing community of Europe's people is driving cross-border cooperation as an integral part of the wider territorial cooperation that has been part of our lives for the last 20 years.
So let us allow this community to give the opening word to this joint report by the two supreme audit institutions of the neighbouring countries:
185 million EU citizens (or 37% of the population) live in border areas.
These areas are usually underdeveloped and peripheral territories, often remnants of the darker parts of Europe's history.
The main objective of cross-border cooperation is to help alleviate the existence of borders as administrative, legal and physical barriers and to further help develop the dormant potential of border regions.
The programmes cover different border regions: some cover sea basins, others inland borders within the EU or borders shared with candidate countries.
Most programs are bilateral, but some involve more than two neighboring countries.
The programmes run for seven years and have a total budget of €5.7 billion from the EU budget. EUR.
The programmes include the following activities: repair and reconstruction of cross-border roads, cycle paths and bridges, investment in cross-border waste management systems, health facilities, research centres, flood control measures, etc., joint management of natural sites or development of tourist destinations, development of common services for the local population, advice on employment issues, creation of thematic networks and clusters for innovation.
Support from the programme is granted on condition that organisations from both sides of the border (such as regional authorities, universities or SMEs) come together to implement projects based on the needs of the border region.
Representatives of cooperating regions, relevant ministries and other local partners meet regularly to decide which projects will be supported.
This system should lead to a sustainable form of cooperation even after the programme itself has come to an end.
Funding is provided on condition that project partners from both sides of the border cooperate in a way that fulfils at least two of the following four features: joint preparation, joint implementation, joint staff, joint funding.
Within the partnership, the tasks and responsibilities for the preparation, implementation, financing and control of the project activities are clearly defined and allocated to the relevant partners.
The main attributes of the partnership include: the joint selection of the lead partner, the joint preparation of the project and the application, the conclusion of a joint partnership agreement signed by all partners, the assumption of overall responsibility for the implementation of the project by the lead partner, the total payments of support are received by the lead partner and transferred to the relevant project partners.
Eligible projects carried out in the territory of regions and voivodeships adjacent to the common border may be supported.
OBJECTIVES AND SCOPE OF CONTROL
On 11 December 2007, the European Commission (EC) approved the Operational Programme of Cross-Border Cooperation Czech Republic-Republic of Poland 2007-2013 (OPPS ČR-POL).
€258 187 467 was earmarked for the implementation of the operational programme.
The total amount included a contribution from the European Funds (ERDF) totalling €219 459 344 (85%), which must be spent by the end of 2015.
Financial data on the use of EU funds from the Operational Programme Cross-Border Cooperation Czech Republic - Republic of Poland 2007 - 2013 at the end of 2013 are presented in the following table:
The cooperation agreement between the supreme audit institutions of the Czech Republic (SAO) and the Republic of Poland (NIK) was concluded by the presidents of both SAIs on 27 February 2013 after previous mutual negotiations at the meeting of the heads of supreme audit institutions of the Visegrad Four, Austria and Slovenia, which took place in September 2012 in Lovasberény, Hungary.
The aim of the cooperation was to examine whether the implementation of the operational programme is carried out in accordance with legal regulations and with binding programming documents or agreements, whether the management and control system works reliably, whether the objectives of the operational programme are achieved and whether the selected projects are implemented in accordance with the conditions of the support provided.
This cooperation took the form of a parallel check, in which the audit authorities independently examined, on the basis of their statutory mandate, subjects the content of which had previously been jointly discussed.
THISsegmentISintentionallyLEFTblank
The audit also included elements of a performance audit.
Selected topics were tested on a sample of projects by project partners on both sides of the border.
As a result of the checks carried out, the audit conclusions were published in the usual manner by both offices.
On the basis of the two audit conclusions set out in the Annex, this Joint Report has been produced.
The selected common control sample comprised six projects (Annex 1) which fall under aid area 1.1 - Strengthening transport accessibility and support area 2.2 - Support for tourism development.
Prior to the start of the audit, the utilization of funds from these two areas of support represented more than 60% of the funds reimbursed to the beneficiaries by the end of 2012 from the ERDF for all implemented projects within the whole OPIE CR-POL.
THISsegmentISintentionallyLEFTblank
In addition to the audit of a common sample of six projects, the SAO audited another 13 projects and the NIK audited two others (all from the support area 1.1 and 2.2).
Funds paid to ERDF beneficiaries audited by the SAO and the NIK amounted to a total of € 25 362 174 (SAO) and € 8 450 784 (NIK).
The audited volume of funds represented 41% of all funds paid to beneficiaries of aid areas 1.1 and 2.2.
MAIN CONTROL CONCLUSIONS
Both audits carried out independently by the SAO on the Czech side and the NIK on the Polish side showed that the level of irregularities identified did not exceed the materiality level.
The implementation of the OPIE CR-POL, including the functioning of the management and control system and the implementation of selected projects, was successful.
The auditees, who were part of the management and control system, took steps to limit the risk of incorrect use of funds and non-use of ERDF funds.
Evidence of the effectiveness of the implemented projects under the Operational Programme is the high level of fulfilment of the set objectives, which was supported by the results of the survey of the social evaluation of project implementation.
SUMMARY OF CONTROL RESULTS
The A. Evaluation of the management and control system
The coordinated approach made it possible to carry out an audit of the management and control system of the OPPS CR-POL implemented by the following institutions in both countries: the managing authority (Ministry for Regional Development of the Czech Republic), the national coordinator (Ministry for Regional Development POL) and the auditors (Centre for Regional Development and Voivodship).
At the same time, the coordinated audit made it possible to evaluate the tasks and functions carried out by the JTS and the Monitoring Committee.
During the evaluation of the management system, the audit focused on the activities carried out in connection with the setting up and performance of the functions of the managing authority and the national coordinator, in particular in relation to the setting of the objectives of the programme and the monitoring of the progress achieved, the evaluation activities and annual reports, the assessment, evaluation, selection and approval of project applications, the use of programme funds and the fulfilment of the obligation to publish correct, complete and up-to-date information on grants, the control activities of the managing authority, the setting of the performance of the activities and the results of the audits of the delegated audit body and the system of coordination and monitoring of irregularities.
Furthermore, the activities of the competent authorities in the area of risk analysis and the establishment of control plans at the site of project implementation were examined and the evaluations of project applications carried out by independent evaluators were assessed.
The examination of the control system included a review of the administrative and on-the-spot checks carried out, which allowed the approval of e.g. supplies of goods and co-financed services, the eligibility of expenditure submitted for reimbursement, as well as the compliance of the disbursement of these funds and related activities (or parts thereof) with national and EU legislation.
Significant shortcomings were detected by the SAO audit in the area of publishing correct, complete and up-to-date information on the provided subsidies.
In the area of project evaluation and approval, the risk of non-transparency of the process was identified.
Other weaknesses were found in the setting of monitoring indicators and in the setting of the irregularity reporting system.
Based on these findings, the Supreme Audit Office (SAO) assessed the control and management system implemented by the MA as partially effective.
Irregularities identified by the NIK concerning the functioning of the management and control system in Poland in relation to violations of the applicable laws and rules were not assessed as significant.
These discrepancies had an impact only on individual projects and did not have a significant impact on the implementation of the whole OPIE CR-POL.
Despite the irregularities identified, the NIK assessed the functioning of the system positively.
B. Evaluation of project results
The audit did not show any discrepancy between the indicators and objectives set for individual projects and those defined by the operational programme.
In all audited projects, the expected results were achieved, the funded activities were implemented and the indicators (measurable parameters) set out in the operational programme project applications were met.
The audit conclusions are in line with the results of the survey of the social evaluation of project implementation described below.
An important aspect to which the SAO and the NIK paid attention in their conclusions is the limited possibility of objective evaluation of the fulfilment of the expected results of individual projects with regard to the objectives of individual projects as well as the objectives of the entire operational programme.
On the initiative of the Supreme Audit Chamber, a public opinion survey was carried out in the summer of 2013 in the place of implementation of projects selected for the joint audit sample.
The survey and its evaluation were carried out by the School of Higher Vocational Education in Nysa (Poland).
In agreement with the Supreme Audit Office, the survey was carried out on both sides of the border between 2,820 respondents, both among the public in Poland in relation to the part of the project implemented by Polish project partners and among the Czech public in relation to the part of the project implemented in the Czech Republic.
The survey mainly covered the suitability, quality, exchange of information, use of funds and the societal function of the projects, including the investments made.
The number of respondents per project is shown in Annex 2.
The examination of the survey responses indicated that the overall social evaluation of the projects was good at 4.8 points (on a scale with a maximum of 8 points), which means that the results of the projects were positively perceived by the majority of respondents both in terms of objectives and other parameters specified in the project applications.
According to the respondents, the projects examined met the criteria expressed in the OP.
Respondents also noted the positive impact of investments on improving the quality of transport and tourist infrastructure, transport accessibility, tourist attractiveness, business conditions, quality of life of citizens and the re-establishment of contacts between the inhabitants of the border region.
On the other hand, the overall evaluation of the responses indicated a certain difference between the actual level of achievement of the objectives/parameters (actual impact rate) and the level expected by the respondents.
This difference applies to the evaluation of the results of individual projects, where in some cases there is a significant difference.
The results of the survey were in line with the opinions of the participants of the panel discussions (target groups) carried out in the framework of the NIK audit on the Polish side, who pointed out the need to implement the projects and confirmed their positive reception by the public and the fulfilment of the expected results.
The C. Evaluation of legality of audited projects
In the case of the beneficiaries, the compliance of the factual and timely implementation of the project with the timetable and content of the project application was examined, the fulfilment of the set objectives and purpose of the project, the fulfilment of binding monitoring indicators, the management of changes in projects, the handling of any project revenues, the fulfilment of the obligation of separate accounting records of project transactions, compliance with the prohibition of financing projects from other EU sources, the implementation of the project partnership and compliance with its conditions, the fulfilment of the mandatory publicity concerning, in particular, the dissemination of awareness of the sources of financing projects from the EU, compliance with the conditions for the eligibility of project expenditures, the selection of contractors and the verification of the delivered performances on the basis of public contracts.
The total financial value of the shortcomings of the SAOs and NIKs qualified as expenditure ineligible for EU funding was found to be €85,094.
However, this amount did not exceed the established materiality level related to the total audited amount of funds for a sample of projects.
Despite the discrepancies, the SAO and the NIK evaluated the implementation of the selected projects by the beneficiaries positively.
In most cases, the commitments under the terms of the aid were properly implemented by the beneficiaries.
The funds earmarked for the implementation of the audited projects were spent in accordance with EU regulations and national law.
However, seven of the eight beneficiaries audited by NIK were found to have irregularities in the fulfilment of material and financial commitments.
These were individual cases of a non-systemic nature and concerned in particular inconsistent preparation of project documentation and breaches of public procurement rules, but did not affect the choice of contractor or the delivery of contracted works.
In only one of all projects audited by the NIK can the irregularity found, which concerned non-compliance with reporting obligations, be classified as ineligible expenditure, amounting to PLN 43 003 (of which EUR 8 231 financed by the ERDF).
On the other side of the border, the SAO identified ineligible expenditures amounting to CZK 2,098,053 in three of the 19 audited projects (of which EUR 76,863 were financed by the ERDF).
Irregularities included non-delivery of paid materials and works or expenditure for the modernisation of a property owned by a person who was not linked to the project either as a beneficiary or as a project partner.
RECOMMENDATIONS
The joint recommendation of the SAO and the NIK concerns the improvement of the monitoring system, especially with regard to the consistent determination of proper monitoring indicators for all specific objectives of the priority axis of the Operational Programme.
In general, efforts should also be made to establish indicators at programme and project level that allow more reliable verification of the achievement of the impacts identified.
Comments and recommendations resulting from inspections carried out on both sides of the border are included in separate audit conclusions of the SAO and the NIK on CD.
COMMUNICATIONS
The report was prepared in Czech, English and Polish and signed by the presidents of the supreme audit institutions of the Czech Republic and the Republic of Poland.
Public works procurement and prevention of corruption
Joint report on parallel audit carried out by the Czech Supreme Audit Office (SAO) and the German Federal Court of Auditors (BRH)
Introduction and overview
Public procurement of goods and services by contracting authorities is very important for both the state and private spheres.
The role of the State is to use public resources as efficiently and economically as possible and to ensure fair and regulated free competition.
In the European Union (EU), public procurement is a key factor for the success of the Single Economic Area.
In its rules, the EU has set thresholds above which the contracting authority must comply with certain requirements to ensure a broad and unrestricted competitive environment across the EU.
EU Member States must therefore transpose European procurement law into their national legislation and put it into practice.
Both the Czech Republic and Germany have already done so.
For public contracts below EU thresholds, the regulation of their award remains a matter for individual countries.
Moreover, public procurement is prone to corruption with a consequent high potential for damage.
Therefore, contracting entities are obliged to take reasonable measures in their procurement procedures to prevent corruption and unfair advantage (corruption prevention).
In January 2011, the Supreme Audit Office of the Czech Republic (Czech SAI - SAO) and the Federal Court of Auditors of Germany (German SAI - BRH) agreed to carry out parallel audits in the area of procurement procedures and prevention of corruption in land and transport constructions.
Cooperation was also focused on the area of European procurement law as transposed into national legislation.
The audits included the awarding of contracts below European thresholds in order to cover the area of corruption prevention.
The audit findings of the two SAIs are summarised in a joint report addressed, inter alia, to international organisations such as the International Organisation of Supreme Audit Institutions (INTOSAI).
Areas of control and institutions audited
The BRH evaluation is based on area-based inspections of the above-mentioned areas carried out at the German building administrations responsible for federal transport and land constructions between 2011 and 2012.
For example, highway construction and large-scale building construction projects were evaluated.
The BRH included findings from past audits in the evaluation.
The SAO's evaluation is based on audits carried out between 2008 and 2012, which focused on road constructions, railway lines, water projects, large buildings (hospitals, cultural facilities, universities) and some other constructions in the Czech Republic.
In Germany, federal public procurement is mostly carried out by the Land Building Administrations.
In the Czech Republic, state institutions for road and rail construction are responsible for awarding public contracts in the field of transport infrastructure.
In the field of building construction, public procurement is carried out by the beneficiaries of the budget.
In both countries, the services of external experts are also used in the various stages of the procurement procedure.
The evaluation should take into account the differences in public procurement legislation and the specificities of the construction market in both countries.
Structure of the Public Procurement Act in both countries
In Germany, the conduct of European procurement procedures is governed by laws and subordinate standards.
Procurement procedures limited to Germany are governed by budgetary law and the relevant administrative acts .
The internal administrative rules contain numerous additional provisions for both European and national procurement procedures.
Further details concerning the requirements of European procurement law and the prevention of corruption are enshrined in the so-called federal government procurement manuals, in which the requirements are elaborated or formulated in greater detail in the light of legal precedents in the field of procurement law.
In the Czech Republic, public procurement is governed by the Public Procurement Act and other legislation concerning contracts with a value above and below European thresholds.
Procurement law underwent substantial changes in 2012.
The new rules have strengthened the transparency of procurement procedures, opened up scope for more competition and reduced opportunities for corruption during the procurement process.
Basic aspects of procurement law in both countries
As regards European procurement procedures, open and restricted procedures in particular need to be applied in both countries.
In Germany, contracts below the European threshold are awarded in accordance with national rules (public call for competition, restricted call for competition, negotiated procedure), but these are practically identical to the above-mentioned conditions for European procurement procedures.
Public procurement in Germany is characterised by the principle of preference for open procedures (where candidates are invited on a European scale) and public calls for participation (only in the case of national procedures).
In open procedures and when calling for competition, any candidate may submit a tender.
Another important feature of German procurement law is the support of small and medium-sized enterprises.
This is mainly achieved by dividing the total public contract into commercially specific lots and subdividing it into subcontracts.
By awarding contracts divided into these lots, as many candidates as possible are given a chance to participate in the procedure.
The two characteristics of German procurement law are time-tested instruments for obtaining good value for money.
More than 330,000 construction companies with 1.9 million employees are registered in Germany.
In the Czech Republic, it is necessary to apply the conditions of European procedures also in cases of public procurement with a value below the EU limit.
An exception is the simplified below-threshold procedure, where the contracting authority may use less onerous procedures.
In accordance with European law, Czech procurement law includes the conditions under which the contract must be awarded in order to ensure competition and achieve a favourable price.
The current Czech legislation allows contracting authorities to choose between an open procedure and a restricted procedure.
There are 85,000 construction companies registered in the Czech Republic, of which about 1,900 have 20 or more employees.
Total volume of public works contracts awarded by contracting entities and share of each type of procedure in both countries
In 2011, the total volume of construction works in Germany amounted to EUR 7 675 billion.
CZK, of which CZK 1 138 billion. CZK (15%) accounted for the share of public contracts, which in addition to contracts awarded by the federal government included contracts awarded by individual Länder and municipalities.
The total volume of federal road and land construction projects (excluding railway and water construction) amounted to CZK 212.5 billion. CZK.
In 2011, the composition of federal contracts for land works and transport works was as follows: 80.7 % open procedures and calls for tender; 12.7 % restricted procedures and calls for tender;
6.6% negotiated procedures and direct procurement; 33.2% is the proportion of European procurement procedures.
The volume of public construction contracts in the Czech Republic reached CZK 118.6 billion in 2011. CZK , which was about 25% of the total volume of all construction works.
In 2009 it amounted to EUR 194.0 billion. CZK, which was 37% of the total.
Structure of types of procurement procedures in 2011: 65,1 % open procedures; 14,3 % restricted procedures; 9,5 % negotiated procedures; 8,4 % simplified procedures for below-threshold contracts; 2,7 % other procedures.
These figures include all public supply, service and works contracts awarded by contracting authorities in 2011.
These figures show that: parallel controls in both countries focused on a substantial part of public procurement procedures; the prevention of corruption, which must always be taken into account in procurement procedures, has a significant financial impact; open importance. procurement procedures are of key importance.
Summary of the main results
The SAO and the BRH carried out parallel audits of European public works contracts with an emphasis on preventing corruption in their respective countries.
The audits focused on the comparison of legal regulations in both countries and the EU and on the implementation of public contracts in the field of land and transport constructions.
The following are the key findings:
The EU Public Procurement Directive has been transposed into the national laws of both countries (points 1. and 3.1).
Preventing corruption is not only an objective of the EU directive, but of the two countries" national procurement rules, which differ partly in focus and detail.
In both countries, the basic requirement to be met by the tender documents is the inclusion of all the information needed for the preparation of tenders.
The specification shall be neutral and shall not contain any requirements restricting competition.
The tender documents in Germany generally meet these requirements.
In the Czech Republic, insufficient definition of the subject matter of the contract is a problem.
In Germany, the division of contracts into lots is required wherever possible in order to promote a competitive environment.
According to the findings of the BRH, this requirement is complied with in Germany.
The SAO audits proved that in the Czech Republic, public works in the field of road construction are awarded without being divided into parts or subdivided, but only into very significant parts. This method may distort competition and lead to a restriction to only a few large companies.
Such restrictions on market potential may result in significant cost increases and risk improper price negotiations and impermissible agreements.
The legislation in both countries allows contracting entities, under certain conditions, to additionally award additional works if this need arises after the conclusion of the contract (point 4.1).
In both countries, the requirements for correct determination of the type of procurement procedure are met.
In the Czech Republic, according to the applicable regulations, contracting entities are free to designate one of the open procurement procedures, namely the open procedure or the restricted procedure.
German procurement and budget law rules enshrine the primacy of open procedures for both European and national tenders, known as public calls for competition.
Open procurement procedures in both countries allow a wider range of candidates to submit bids and also reduce the risk of corruption by making it more difficult to negotiate prices illegally between candidates.
At the same time, the best value for money is achieved.
For large contracts with an estimated total price reaching or exceeding the thresholds set by the EU, the contracting authorities audited have always launched a European procurement procedure.
Correct determination of the expected value of the contract plays a very important role in determining the type of procurement procedure.
In Germany, databases are used to objectively determine the price of public works, in which values are based on completed construction projects.
This is a sufficiently objective instrument.
In the Czech Republic, databases are only used in the field of road construction but are not yet an effective tool (point 4.2).
When awarding a works contract under European procurement procedures, it is necessary to publish preliminary information and a contract notice in order to strengthen competition by allowing candidates to prepare themselves in good time for the submission of tenders.
As a rule, the construction authorities audited complied with this requirement.
In isolated cases, errors in the information released, incorrect application of provisions and/or a lack of controls may have impaired competition (item 4.3).
There is a greater risk of manipulation when selecting candidates to participate in a restricted procurement procedure.
Under EU law, the number of candidates invited must be high enough to guarantee genuine competition.
The selection criteria shall be objective, non-discriminatory and published well in advance.
Beyond EU requirements, contracting entities in both countries should rotate as often as possible the candidates they invite to participate in the procedure when selecting suppliers.
In Germany, there are partly very detailed requirements to prevent the risks of manipulation.
In some cases, the contracting authorities audited did not comply with these requirements.
In the Czech Republic, selection decisions were not always credible (paragraph 4.4).
In both countries, contracting entities must ensure during the procurement procedure that all candidates wishing to submit a tender receive the same information.
Any agreements between those candidates and between them and third parties involved in the preparation of the specifications must, as far as possible, be prevented from restricting competition by means of illegal agreements.
In Germany, the measures are more thorough than in the Czech Republic.
However, the German contracting authorities audited did not meet this requirement in several cases (point 4.5).
In both countries, the principle of cross-checking and transparency must be applied when opening envelopes.
The labelling, documentation, protection and confidentiality requirements for tenders are intended to limit the scope for manipulation and are particularly detailed in Germany.
Checks in both countries found only a few cases of irregularities (paragraph 4.6).
The assessment and evaluation of bids is procedurally regulated in both countries.
The relevant German measures are more comprehensive but have not always been respected by the audited contracting authorities.
For example, contracting entities transmitted their tenders to third parties for inspection without their prior assessment, without adequately documenting any suspicious circumstances.
The tenders were thus insufficiently protected against subsequent manipulation (section 4.7).
For reasons of legal protection enterprises are to be informed about the intended contract award before the latter is finalised.
This legal protection rule is also designed to prevent corruptive action.
The law of the Czech Republic provides for judicial review of contract award procedures also for national tenders.
Only in few cases, the notification of the enterprises was not appropriately ensured (item 4.8).
The procurement documentation of the audited contracting authorities in both countries was not always complete or fully credible.
In the case of several procurement procedures, the transparency requirements applied at European level were not always properly respected in the minutes of the proceedings.
Moreover, some of the weaknesses identified reduced the preventive anti-corruption function of the documentation.
Contracting entities in both countries mostly complied with their reporting obligations (paragraph 4.9).
Summary of guiding principles of European procurement law and prevention of corruption
European procurement law
In order to ensure free competition in the EU, the European Parliament and the Council of the European Union adopted, inter alia, a Directive on the coordination of procedures for the award of public works contracts, public supply contracts and public service contracts with a view to ensuring the free movement of goods and services and a Directive on the coordination of laws, regulations and administrative provisions relating to review procedures for the award of public supply contracts and public works contracts (the Remedies Directive), which were to be transposed into national law.
The general EU procurement principles include in particular: the requirement for a competitive environment; the requirement for equal treatment or non-discrimination; the requirement for transparency.
EU procurement principles include In addition, the Directive on the coordination of public procurement procedures includes provisions for construction projects with a total estimated value of €5 million or more.
These provisions affect: the content of the specifications; the type of procurement procedure used; the publication of certain information before, during and after the procedure; compliance with the deadline for receipt of tenders; the qualification requirements that may be required of tenderers; the criteria for selecting the most suitable tender.
The legal protection of tenderers is governed by the Remedies Directive.
In the event of a potential breach of the procurement right, tenderers may have the contracting authority's decision reviewed in accordance with the review procedures.
Both countries have transposed EU requirements into national legislation.
Prevention of corruption
The importance of preventing corruption
In neither country is there a legal definition of what corruption actually means.
Corruption is generally understood as the abuse of a personal (confidential) position for one's own benefit.
As a rule, this means that such abuse is encouraged by the beneficiaries.
Corruption can hinder competition and cause serious economic losses.
Overall corruption-related damage is estimated to amount to 5% of world economic output.
For the EU area, these costs are estimated at 1% of the EU's economic output.
Where a contracting authority is involved, agreements restricting competition or allowing unfair conduct may undermine confidence in the integrity, capability and overall work of the public administration.
This is why preventing corruption is a key task for contracting authorities.
In particular, practices and procedures need to be checked in areas particularly susceptible to corruption in order to take adequate measures to prevent corruption.
Taking preventive measures and carrying out effective checks can substantially reduce the vulnerability of the decision-making process to corruption.
Public works procurement is generally considered to be an area particularly vulnerable to corruption.
Measures in the field of prevention of corruption
In Germany, corruption is prosecutable under criminal and labour law, in particular active and passive bribery, receiving and offering significant benefits.
The German federal government has issued a manual with recommendations on the prevention of corruption in the federal administration, according to which the following principles are applied throughout the procurement process outside the risk analysis: the priority of open procurement; the principle of multiple control and transparent decision-making; the separation of the planning, procurement and accounting phases; accurate and complete procurement documentation.
In addition, other preventive measures against corruption have become part of the procurement law.
In 2011, the relevant Federal Ministry of Information Technology introduced a system to monitor procurement for the construction of federal roads.
With this system, data such as estimated costs, the total value of the contract and the involvement of private design offices can be fully recorded and analysed in order to identify any discrepancies.
In the Czech Republic, corruption is prosecuted under criminal law, including on the level of labour and civil law.
The legal requirement for transparency, equal treatment and non-discrimination throughout the procurement process (preparation and implementation) is the most important part of preventing corruption.
Anti-corruption measures in this area were set out by the government in the "Government Anti-Corruption Strategy 2011-2012," which was updated for 2013 and 2014.
Part of these measures was incorporated into the Public Procurement Act and related implementing regulations.
The new legislation contains the following basic provisions: reduction of the thresholds for small-scale contracts; obligation of prior notification by the contracting authority, including the reasons for the contract; prohibition of limiting the number of tenderers in the restricted procedure and abolition of the general possibility to limit the number of tenderers by drawing lots; obligation to publish contracts, including annexes; obligation to publish actual and lists of subcontractors.
Results of inspections relating to subsequent stages of the procurement procedure
Tender documents
The specifications shall contain all the information necessary to enable tenderers to prepare their tenders in an appropriate manner.
In particular, the documentation must make it possible to process the tender price.
For this to be possible, candidates must not be exposed to risks which they could not have anticipated.
The specifications shall include, in particular, the specifications, the requirements for demonstrating the qualifications of the tenderers and the criteria for selecting the most advantageous tender.
Requirements of European law and prevention of corruption
European law contains, in particular, requirements concerning technical specifications.
The specifications must restrict competition as little as possible and all candidates must have equal access to them.
The specification shall be neutral.
Requirements for specific specific products are admissible on a very limited scale in the procurement documents.
The basic principle of preventing corruption is that tender documents do not contain requirements that could restrict competition.
Furthermore, documentation should avoid ambiguities or inaccurate data which create scope for speculation and manipulation in the evaluation of tenders.
Provisions of German and Czech procurement law
Identical or similar provisions
Both countries have transposed EU legal provisions into their national legislation.
The basic principle is that specifications should be given in general terms without reference to a specific product or brand.
The specifications shall define precisely the supplies and services required and detail the number of units of work and supply.
In normal cases, prices should be given by unit of labour or supply (e.g. in CZK/m or in CZK/item).
Only in exceptional cases is it permissible to define the required works in terms of their functionality.
In principle, specifications must be drawn up in a complete and unambiguous manner.
They must contain precise quantities and must not include works and supplies the necessity of which is not entirely obvious.
Reimbursement in the form of lump sums or time worked should be avoided where possible.
These parts of the procurement documents shall exclude speculation and manipulation based on ambiguous specifications.
The specifications must enable each tenderer to prepare its tender with certainty and without excessive preparatory work.
The invitation to tender must be published only when the specifications are complete.
Different provisions
In the Czech Republic, there is no obligation for the contracting authority to award construction contracts in instalments.
A contracting authority may divide a contract into several separate lots where the nature of the subject-matter of such a contract so permits.
In Germany, the application of the rule of dividing works into lots is required, either quantitatively (sub-supplies) or according to specific considerations (specialised lots).
This allows SMEs to participate in the competition, increases the number of potential bidders and makes price fixing more difficult.
This type of award is usually considered to be the most appropriate in terms of providing value for money.
In the Czech Republic, procurement documents must include evaluation criteria in national and European procurement procedures.
This should inform the tenderer comprehensively and exhaustively of the criteria against which its tender will be evaluated.
In Germany, the requirement to include evaluation criteria in the notice applies only to European calls for tender.
In national procurement procedures, evaluation criteria need only be included in the contract record, which is an internal administrative document.
Where the tender documents are prepared by authorised independent experts, German regulations require the exclusion of any information which would help to identify specific interested firms, architects or designers.
This is to prevent individual construction companies from obtaining any additional information or other impermissible competitive advantages without the contracting authority's knowledge through contacts with these independent experts.
This is also the reason why independent experts are not allowed to publish documents relating to tenders and to make them available for screening.
The client is a one-stop shop for construction companies.
The Czech Republic does not currently have a similar provision.
In the case of large construction projects, there is almost always a need for additional work or modification of existing works after the conclusion of the original contract.
The legislation of both countries allows the award of a multi-works contract up to a certain threshold to the same supplier without the need to publish and carry out a new procurement procedure.
The volume of additional work under both European and German legislation may not exceed 50% of the original value of the contract.
Moreover, the additional work must be necessary and must be specific to the main contract.
In the Czech Republic, a stricter limit of 20% is applied for the award of additional works.
This may risk a greater time lag in the implementation of the contract and the possible integration of a new entity into an already running project.
Where an additional works contract is awarded to the supplier of the original contract, German law contains a provision which prevents suppliers from arbitrarily increasing the price of the modified works or additional works.
The calculations must be made taking into account the prices agreed in the original contract.
In addition to additional work, less work may occur during construction, i.e. jobs that weren't needed.
In the Czech Republic, less work and more work cannot be compensated for each other.
Key control findings
The BHR
Technical specifications and product-neutral invitation to tender
In isolated cases, the specifications referred to national technical requirements or standards, but these have since been replaced by EU standards.
In some cases, the specifications included particulars requiring a particular brand name, e.g. for a public sign resistance system, to prevent water penetration or for noise protection cladding, although this was not necessary.
In some cases, the specifications were so detailed that only one product met the requirements.
Quantity specifications divided into items
The BRH did not observe non-conformity with the general requirements for the invitation to tender for supplies and services according to the quantity specifications divided into lots in a single case of inspection of the procurement procedures.
Completeness of specifications
In most of the cases audited, the tender documents were fully prepared before the call for tenders was launched.
However, in various cases, documents had to be modified during the procurement procedure when changes in appropriations or in the timetable had to be taken into account.
In rare cases, the tender documents included works and supplies whose need was uncertain.
One tender dossier for demolition work contained almost exclusively such items.
Division of the contract and award criteria
In many cases, the required justification for not splitting deliveries into lots has not been demonstrated.
The BRH did not find any shortcomings in the criteria for evaluating tenders prior to the award of the contract.
Inclusion of the names of authorised independent experts
Interested authorised experts were mentioned in many of the documents.
The names appeared on the opening pages of the documents or on the plans or annexed opinions.
In one case, the contracting authority even invited tenderers to contact an explicitly appointed architect with their questions on the procurement procedure.
In the case of ground construction projects, these cases were found mainly in the tender documentation, where self-employed experts were entrusted with the processing of the specifications.
The contracting authorities indicated that they did not have sufficient staffing capacity to examine the tender documents in detail.
SAO
Technical specifications and product-neutral invitation to tender
In the case of a public contract for the construction of a house with 12 flats, reference was made directly to specific manufacturers and product brands.
The sponsor did not accept the use of other similar product types or justify why it required the supply of specific products.
For the construction of university facilities, the tender documentation included references to specific business companies and specific product designations.
General overview of specifications and partial specifications
In the case of the construction of certain industrial zones, the contracting authority did not unequivocally define in the tender documents the quantity and type of works, supplies or services required in the statement of assessment and set only the built-up area in cubic metres for each building as a unit of measurement.
Completeness of specifications
During the procurement procedure for the construction of the ring road, the specifications for the restricted procedure were amended four times between August and September 2009.
This was due to changes in the project documentation caused by the extension of the scope of the contract and the change in technical conditions.
In the case of the construction of social buildings, the tender documentation had defects that forced the creation of additional works (there was a lack of precise focus, insufficient structural and technical survey was carried out, drawings were missing).
In the tender documents for the construction of the large library, the interior elements were mentioned only in a descriptive manner, without specifying the qualitative requirements for the products.
The supply of the interior equipment of the building was provided by subcontractors who purchased some above-standard and very expensive equipment.
Lots and award criteria
In the Czech Republic there is no obligation to award the contract in parts.
An analysis of three large road constructions during the construction of the ring road showed that their award in an open procedure in parts (separately for selected buildings) could achieve savings of up to CZK 790 million from the achieved price of public contracts of CZK 15,508 million.
For example, with the award of six construction buildings as a separate part of the public contract, savings of CZK 62.5 million could have been achieved.
CZK, i.e. 25% of the contracted price and if 20 bridges were awarded as a separate part of the public contract, savings of CZK 287.8 million could be achieved, i.e. also 25%.
When constructing the roads, the contracting authority set out in the tender documents qualification criteria which limited the participation of small suppliers in the tendering procedure.
In the terms and conditions of the open procedure per supplier for one construction of the motorway, the contracting authority required, among other things, the contractor's practice of implementing at least three road constructions with a financial volume of more than CZK 700 million each, completed or put into operation within the last five years.
There are only nine companies in the Czech Republic that meet the condition of completion of constructions over CZK 700 million in the last five years.
The public contract for the delivery of another motorway construction in an open procedure included six separate constructions for which the contracting authority required a minimum annual volume of construction works in the last three years of CZK 12 billion.
Two thirds of financial volume of road construction works (i.e. €2.5 billion out of €3.7 billion) in the period of 2008-2009 were realized by 5 suppliers or individually either in form of consortium.
For 46 contracts for a total of EUR 13.2 billion There were only two or even one bidder.
The SAO pointed out that limiting market potential can lead to a large increase in costs.
Multitask Area
In two cases, the contracting authority did not conclude a written contract for the supply of additional works and did not assign additional works to any of the procedures specified in the Act.
The value of unapproved additional works in one case was CZK 1,591.4 million without VAT (21% increase) and in the other case CZK 4,135.8 million without VAT (91% increase).
The cause of the additional work was mainly an ambiguous definition of the amount and type of work required.
In the case of the construction of university facilities, the contracting authority did not include in the tender documentation some works and supplies whose necessity for completion and final approval of the construction was already known when the contract was awarded.
For these works and supplies, he concluded amendments to the contract for work with the construction contractor with an increase in price, in a negotiated procedure without publication, even though the volume of additional works exceeded the national limit of 20% of the value of the original contract.
In connection with the construction of the Palace of Justice, the contracting authority reserved the right to award additional works without a procurement procedure.
During the construction, the contracting authority increased the scope of the contract by CZK 50.5 million.
The unit prices of the additional works determined without competitive tendering were significantly higher than comparable works carried out by the same contractor.
Other findings
On a selected sample of constructions, the SAO found that the average construction costs for the construction of one kilometre of a four-lane motorway in the Czech Republic amounted to CZK 355.0 million.
According to long-term data obtained from the German Ministry of Construction, the average cost in Germany per kilometre section of the four-lane motorway is CZK 260.0 million.
This comparison suggests that the acquisition of a motorway in the Czech Republic is more costly than in Germany.
Summary
The legal provisions on tender documents are almost identical in both countries and are in line with the requirements of European law.
The aim is to ensure full and fair competition and prevention of corruption.
The specifications must be sufficiently precise to enable candidates to calculate costs and prepare their tenders.
Moreover, this requirement limits the scope for speculation and protects the contracting authority against the need to award additional works.
In Germany, the basic legal provisions on tender documents are complemented by additional rules to further reduce the possibilities for restricting competition and engaging in manipulation.
Although there are no detailed requirements of this kind in the Czech Republic, greater transparency is achieved by publishing the award criteria.
In both countries the specification requirements are mostly met.
However, in some cases shortcomings have been identified which could lead to a restriction of competition.
The currently applicable rule in Germany requiring bulky construction projects to be subdivided into lots seems appropriate for strengthening the competitive environment and achieving cost-effective results.
The SAO found that in the Czech Republic, the award of contracts for large-scale projects without partial award or division into large-scale parts may limit the range of candidates to a small number of large suppliers.
The result of the strict requirement for references of candidates, which is applied in the Czech Republic for the selection of candidates for road construction works, is the fact that public contracts are awarded only to a small group of applicants and therefore small and medium-sized companies have no chance to compete.
The limited number of bidders also increases the risk of price fixing and other illegal agreements.
As regards the award and payment of additional contracts for additional works or modified works, regulatory approaches differ between the two countries.
If the value of the additional works under European and German law does not exceed 50% of the value of the original contract, the additional works may be awarded without a procurement procedure and its announcement.
In the Czech Republic, a stricter limit of 20% applies.
THISsegmentISintentionallyLEFTblank
This limit is often exceeded by the contracting authorities.
THISsegmentISintentionallyLEFTblank
If the limit of 20% is exceeded, additional works must be awarded on the basis of a new procurement procedure.
Dividing works into parts can avoid restricting competition and the consequent adverse consequences.
Determination of the type of procurement procedure with regard to EU thresholds
By determining the type of procurement procedure, the contracting authority defines the range of potential suppliers that will be admitted to the competition.
The contracting authority may leave the scope of potential suppliers unlimited or may limit the scope according to the criteria specified.
In the case of major project works with a value reaching or exceeding the thresholds set by the EU, a European procurement procedure must be conducted.
Requirements of European law and prevention of corruption
In choosing the type of procedure, the contracting authority must comply in particular with the requirements of European law relating to the competitive environment and transparency.
If the estimate of the total cost of the contract for the required works reaches or exceeds the current EU limit of €5.0 million (CZK 125 million), an open or restricted procurement procedure at European level is always required.
In exceptional cases, a negotiated procedure or a competitive dialogue is admissible.
If the construction project reaches the EU limit, the contracting authority may deviate to a limited extent from the European procurement procedure by launching a national procedure if the total value of the relevant part is below the threshold of €1.0 million (CZK 25 million) and the total value of all parts does not exceed 20% of the estimated value of the entire contract.
Splitting a building project or deliberately estimating its cost too low with the intention of avoiding the fundamental obligation to launch a European call for tenders is not permissible.
The choice of management type is very important for preventing corruption, as restricting competition substantially increases the risk of manipulation.
Therefore, a detailed justification of the chosen type of procedure, in particular in relation to the obligation to apply European procurement law, is an important part of the tender documentation.
Provisions of German and Czech procurement law
Identical or similar provisions
Both countries have fully implemented EU legal requirements for procurement procedures and thresholds in their national legislation.
Different provisions
The Czech Republic has introduced the principle of equal validity of open and restricted procedures also for procurement procedures at national level.
Open and restricted procedures can always be chosen, while other types of procedure are allowed only if the conditions laid down by law are met.
Unlike the Czech legislation, the relevant German regulation of budgetary law and the principles of corruption prevention prefers open procedures or a public invitation to participate in the procedure.
Other types of proceedings, including restricted proceedings, are admissible only exceptionally, where permitted by law.
Such exemptions shall be justified and documented.
The principle of preference for open procedures is intended to ensure that restrictions of competition are avoided as far as possible and that opportunities for manipulation (pre-selected specific candidates) are limited.
In Germany, this principle of public procurement is central to the prevention of corruption.
A correct estimation of the expected contract price is an important basis for deciding on the appropriate type of procurement procedure.
In estimating the costs of building projects, contracting authorities in Germany can rely on the budget and cost databases of public administrations and other organisations.
These were compiled on the basis of an analysis of completed construction projects and include, among other things, standard cost values for types of construction, construction units, construction parts and partial construction works.
On the basis of such standard cost values, it is possible to estimate the contract value of the planned project or works.
Only a few building administrations in Germany already have a similar database for road construction.
However, it is expected that the database will be extended at national level.
Contracting entities in the Czech Republic do not use any central database or average prices of comparable objects to determine the expected costs of public works contracts.
In many cases, the determination of costs is based on an estimate by the entity financing the project or on the value of the resources available to that entity.
Measures for standardisation are still at an early stage.
On the basis of the Anti-Corruption Programme 2011-2012, the relevant ministries are to draw up a database on customary prices for standardised commodities.
The use of a price system to determine the expected price of the contract is determined by a new regulation ordering that the subject-matter of the contract be specified in detail.
The pricing system should serve as a collection of information on construction and assembly work, materials and products and other data in order to calculate the necessary costs and to establish unit prices.
The Ministry of Transport has issued normative prices for transport constructions for the purpose of their control, which are regularly updated.
The database is intended to determine the price of the project (investment) plan and should play a comparative function in the next stages of the preparation of construction works.
Key BRH control findings
Choice of procurement procedure
The audited contracting authorities generally awarded contracts in an open procedure or in the form of a public call for participation and thus complied with the requirements of budgetary and procurement legislation.
In 2011, the share of public tenders in the total volume of federal construction projects in the field of civil engineering was 59.5%.
In road construction, the share was 94.8%.
The BRH found that in some cases the use of non-public competition was not sufficiently justified.
In the case of a blanket check, the BRH found, on the basis of information collected from more than 16 000 proceedings, that a restricted procedure has a clear disadvantage compared to a public procedure and a public challenge, and limited procedures also increase the risk of manipulation and corruption.
THISsegmentISintentionallyLEFTblank
According to the BRH study, the bid prices in the restricted and negotiated procedures were between 6% and 10% higher than in the public procedures.
Parallel studies by the Ministry of Construction and the Provincial Audit Court confirmed the differences in value for money obtained in public and restricted proceedings.
A study by the Ministry of Construction even found that prices in restricted procedures were twice as high as prices in public procedures.
Compliance with EU limits
For projects whose total contract value was estimated at more than €5 million (CZK 125 million), the audited contracting authorities usually prepared procurement plans stating which works would be awarded in the European tender and which in the national tender.
According to the procurement plans, the proportion of works awarded under European rules has always exceeded 80% of the total works.
There has been no circumvention of the requirement for European management in the form of an exorbitantly low cost estimate.
However, in some cases the minimum share for pan-European management was not reached due to a calculation error.
In the course of carrying out the works, some contracting entities have illegally amended the contract allocation plan.
They did not provide any justification for these changes and did not demonstrate that the minimum proportion of European proceedings would subsequently be complied with.
The proportion of European procedures based on project accounts was generally lower than that provided for in the procurement plan.
If the expected contract values of the construction projects exceeded the European limits, the contracting authorities did not use the European procedure for all individual partial parts exceeding €1 million (CZK 25 million).
Instead, they awarded the relevant contracts on the basis of a national procedure without giving valid reasons for the need to derogate from the requirement.
SAO
Selection of the type of procurement procedure
In the case of several large construction projects (e.g. the construction of a road section, facilities for a major sporting event and the construction of apartments), the contracting authority divided one project into several lots, although these subcontracts were of the same or similar nature.
The split reduced the expected value of the contract and the contracts were awarded under a less demanding regime or even outside the statutory regime.
Methods of calculating the estimated price
To date, the price standards applicable to road construction projects have not been an effective tool for determining the expected price.
They were based on prices reported in completed proceedings, which were, however, burdened by inefficiencies as well as excessive profits of suppliers.
The SAO found that actual prices for three parts of the ring road were approximately 37% lower than these price standards.
The Ministry of Transport is to update the standards in relation not only to prices from the past, but also to other comparable markets and price developments.
For buildings, the projected price was based on the project budget carried out by the architects or on an estimate by the investor.
The actual price then differed significantly from the expected price.
The estimated price of the construction of the large library was twice as high as the contractual price, but during the implementation it increased by CZK 54 million, i.e. by 27 per cent.
Similarly, for the construction of the depository of this library, the contract price was 33% lower than the anticipated price.
In other cases, the price of the successful applicant was higher than the estimated price and increased further in the course of implementation.
In the case of the construction of a large library, it was by CZK 400 million, i.e. by 22%. In the case of the construction of the palace of justice, the actual price increased disproportionately during the construction (by 139%) compared to the increase in functional areas (by only 12%).
Summary
Both countries have implemented European provisions on the choice of the type of procurement procedure and on respecting European thresholds in their national legislation, so that the respective national regulations of both countries are almost identical.
However, the principle of public proceedings in Germany is stricter than the European regulation and the current national regulation in the Czech Republic.
Compared to a restricted procurement procedure, a public procedure or a public call for tenders gives more competitors the opportunity to participate in the procurement procedure.
These types of procurement procedures generate a large number of bids that guarantee the best value for money.
The prevention of corruption is significantly strengthened, as the pool of candidates is not limited by decisions taken in advance by the contracting authority, which thus gives scope for manipulation and price fixing between bidders.
The studies confirmed the advantages of the German provision on the preference for open procedures or public calls for participation.
Czech law allows contracting entities to award contracts in open or restricted procedures.
However, the latest legislation of the Public Procurement Act introduces a prohibition for contracting authorities to limit the number of tenderers in restricted procedures.
The restricted procedure has now come very close to the open procedure.
In Germany, the requirement of preference for an open procedure or a public call for competition is largely met by contracting entities, while in the Czech Republic some projects have been subdivided in breach of the law in order to circumvent the requirement to choose the appropriate type of procurement procedure.
However, for some projects carried out in Germany, the European thresholds were not respected, resulting in a minimum share not being reached in pan-European procedures.
In the Czech Republic, a satisfactory system has not yet been put in place to facilitate the correct calculation of the expected costs of construction works and projects.
Where appropriate, contracting entities should ensure, through more extensive checks, that the requirements for the choice of the type of procurement procedure and the volume of contracts awarded in European procedures are met or should create the conditions necessary to allow the correct calculation of expected costs.
Preliminary information and notice of initiation
In cases where contracts are awarded under a European procedure, the contracting authority must publish a report on the procurement procedure.
Prior information and contract notices are an important basis for European competition.
Requirements of European law and prevention of corruption
By means of indicative notices, the contracting authority must indicate the basic specifications of the projects envisaged.
Prior notices are required for projects where the estimate of the total cost is equal to or greater than the European threshold and where the contracting authority wishes to shorten the time limit for the submission of tenders in individual procurement procedures.
The contracting authority is obliged to publish a contract notice with detailed information for all public contracts requiring a European procedure.
Once published, the information shall be binding on the contracting authority.
The notice shall include, inter alia, specifications, time limits for the management and completion of the contract, specific requirements and selection criteria and their weightings.
A common procurement vocabulary must be used to describe the subject of the contract.
In the EU, preliminary information and contract notices are published in the Official Journal of the EU and on the profile of the contracting entity.
In order to avoid discrimination against foreign candidates, notices at national level are not to contain any additional information and are not to be published before the text of the notice is sent to the Publications Office of the EU.
Incomplete or inaccurate pre-notification information may distort competition or even facilitate manipulation.
Provisions of German and Czech procurement law
Identical or similar provisions
Both countries have fully transposed the EU provisions on prior information and contract notices into their national legislation.
Different provisions
