In the Czech Republic, preliminary information and notices must also be published in the case of contracts for which a European procedure is not required.
The procurement procedure may not be initiated earlier than one month after dispatch of the indicative notice.
Disclosure of prior information may be waived only in exceptional cases.
In Germany, prior information is not required in cases where there is no need for a European procedure.
In the case of national proceedings, the public call must be published on the federal government website and, where appropriate, in the press.
In the case of restricted procedures without competition from participants, the notice must be published if the estimated price of the contract is equal to or higher than €25,000 (CZK 625,000).
Prior notifications must explain all the planned parts of the project.
This is an important requirement in view of the number of small orders in Germany.
The division of projects into smaller parts is not so frequent in the Czech Republic and this measure is considered less important.
Key control findings
BRHs
Prior notification
The contracting authorities published a pre-notice of projects which included works to be awarded under the European procedure.
In some open procedures, they reduced the time limit, although the number of days between the sending of the pre-notice and the sending of the contract notice was less than 52 days.
In the case of other accelerated procedures, the annual time-limits for prior information had expired and should have been renewed.
In many cases, the pre-notification did not indicate the envisaged division of the works into several parts.
Contract notices
Some notifications contained codes from the common dictionary which deviated from the content of the prior notification or did not specify precisely the work to be carried out.
In rare cases, notifications at national level included more information, e.g. on deadlines, or were published before a notice was published in the Official Journal of the EU.
Ex-ante transparency
One contracting entity hired an external company to publish a national notice.
It posted a notice on its platform, the use of which was subject to a fee.
However, the sponsor did not check whether the company had to comply with the requirement to send a notice to a free online government portal.
The sample found that only a third of the notices were actually published on the government portal.
SAO
Contract notices
At the time of the construction of the waste water treatment plant, the contracting authority did not lay down in the contract award notice the criteria according to which it would reduce a large number of tenders for the evaluation procedure.
The method of selection (reduction) of tenders for evaluation was not substantiated by the contracting authority.
The number of bids was reduced by previous legislation.
During the construction of the ring road, the contracting authority did not define in the notice of initiation of the restricted procedure or in the qualification documentation the values according to which it will assess the degree of fulfilment of the qualification criteria or the number of tenderers to be invited.
The contracting authority evaluated the fulfilment of the qualification in such a way that all six candidates in the restricted procedure met it, but excluded one of them, saying that it met only the last sixth place.
The procedure of the sponsor was not transparent as it did not publish in advance information on which of the qualification data would be evaluated and how.
Publication of calls
In the case of building modifications in former military retreats, the contracting authority did not publish a written invitation to tender in violation of the law.
Conclusions and recommendations/summary
A two-stage information process with prior information and the publication of a notice of initiation is an important tool for meeting the requirements of transparency and strengthening the competitive environment in European procedures.
By extending the obligation to publish preliminary information in the Czech Republic also for national procurement procedures and by strengthening this obligation in Germany for restricted procedures, both countries have increased the transparency of procurement procedures and improved the competitive environment.
Selection of candidates in restricted procurement procedures
Unlike an open procedure, restricted procurement procedures do not allow all potential interested tenderers to participate.
In restricted procedures, the procuring entity shall first select potential suppliers to be asked to submit a tender.
Requirements of European law and prevention of corruption
EU provisions allow limiting the number of candidates in European restricted procedures.
However, the restricted procedure requires at least five tenderers to be invited to submit a tender and, in the case of a negotiated procedure with publication, at least three potential tenderers must be invited.
In any event, the number of candidates invited must be sufficiently large to guarantee genuine competition.
The selection criteria shall be objective and non-discriminatory and shall be published in advance.
The selection of potential candidates to be invited is highly susceptible to manipulation, e.g. the preferences of some of them.
Reducing competition to a few bidders may ease their collusion.
This has a negative impact on the outcome of the proceedings.
Therefore, the contracting authority must take measures to prevent manipulation and ensure that it has effective control so that the procedure is transparent and credible.
Provisions of German and Czech procurement law
Identical or similar provisions
The provisions for selecting candidates are similar in both countries.
In addition to the requirements of the European Directive, contracting entities should replace the tenderers they invite to submit a tender.
Nor is it permissible to restrict competition from a regional point of view.
Contracting entities must document and justify the selection of tenderers and the selection process.
Different provisions
In Germany, at least three candidates are required to be invited in the restricted procedure and several suitable candidates in the negotiated procedure.
With the exception of two cases, the new Czech regulations do not allow limiting the number of candidates addressed in the case of a restricted and negotiated procedure.
In Germany, the "four-eyes principle" must be respected when selecting candidates.
While one worker proposes a list of candidates, another must substantially modify the list, e.g. by deleting the proposed candidate or by installing another.
This is intended to avoid a situation in which an individual can definitively determine the pool of candidates to participate in the procedure.
In the absence of such a provision, far-reaching illegal collusion between the parties is possible.
The list of tenderers invited to submit a tender must be confidential and must not be divulged.
Authorised independent experts are also obliged to do so.
In the Czech Republic, there is no similar measure or measure for the confidential treatment of the list of candidates.
Under the German provisions, they may not designate candidates from an external company, but may submit proposals to the contracting authority.
There is no similar requirement in the Czech Republic.
Key control findings
BRHs
Number of candidates
Apart from a few cases, the requirement to reach a minimum number of candidates was met.
An analysis of almost 7 000 contracts for works with a value up to the national threshold showed that in the case of restricted procedures the contracting authorities invited an average of 6.6 potential bidders and in the case of direct negotiated procedures an average of 4.4 bidders.
Switching suppliers and regional constraints
In particular, in the area of building construction, the contracting authorities did not change the range of interviewed bidders because they had good experience with those with whom they had cooperated so far.
THISsegmentISintentionallyLEFTblank
The contracting authorities argued that, in the case of a non-public procedure, they should guarantee to approach tenderers whose suitability they had already ascertained.
Although there was no evidence for illegal regional restrictions, the pool of bidders in the closed procedure included a larger proportion of regional bidders than in the case of public tenders.
This was explained by the low level of the contract compared to the public tender and the high travel costs for potential bidders.
Principle of cross-checking and suggestions of self-employed professionals
For the most part, contracting authorities ignored the obligation to amend the list of candidates proposed by self-employed professionals.
They argued in particular that there was a lack of staff, time pressure or inadequate knowledge of the suitable tenderer.
In many cases, the records did not show who had proposed the candidate who had been asked to participate in the procedure.
A check of some samples showed that the list of potential candidates was drawn up by self-employed professionals and accepted by the contracting authority without changes.
As a result, the contracting authorities did not sufficiently protect themselves against potential manipulation.
Confidentiality of the treatment of lists of potential candidates
One contracting authority maintained a list of candidates for invitation to tender in a database to which all staff had access at all times.
This allowed manipulation or unauthorised disclosure of the names of the presumed or accepted bidders, who could then agree on a price.
Documentation
The documentation gave reasons for the selection of candidates only in a few cases.
SAO
Criteria for selecting candidates
During the construction of the ring road, candidates did not have information about which of the data on their qualifications would be evaluated and how.
For the action relating to the development and modernisation of waterways and ports, the tender notice did not set criteria for limiting the number of tenderers.
All eight candidates met the qualifications, but this exceeded the number to which the contracting authority limited participation in the procurement procedure.
The Commission then selected the tenderers by random selection - by drawing lots - which was legal at the time.
That procedure should have been mentioned as a criterion in the call for tenders.
Summary
In view of the high risk of manipulation in the selection of the winning bidder in restricted procurement procedures, both countries have introduced special measures to prevent the illegal restriction of competition.
German rules limit such opportunities for manipulation by means of so-called cross-checking (four-eyes principle), by keeping the list of candidates confidential and by prohibiting proposals for the selection of candidates to external companies.
Czech rules aim to prevent potential restrictions with the aim of manipulation mainly by an unlimited number of bidders.
However, the audit found that a part of the above-mentioned rules was not applied to a sufficient extent and that an inadmissible restriction of the range of candidates was not prevented in some cases.
The contracting authorities are obliged to completely eliminate the mentioned deficiencies.
Submission of tenders
The procurement phase, in which potential tenderers can draw up tenders on the basis of the tender documents they have received, begins with the publication of information on the procurement procedure and/or an invitation to tenderers.
Until the date on which the envelopes are opened, potential tenderers may submit their tenders to the contracting authority in order to bid for the award of the contract.
Requirements of European law and prevention of corruption
EU law does not prescribe specific requirements at this stage of the procurement procedure.
The contracting entity must follow the national rules governing this stage of the procedure and avoid potential tampering.
Those provisions relate in particular to organisation and control.
Provisions of German and Czech procurement law
Identical or similar provisions
The principle is that tender documents on the basis of which potential suppliers prepare their tenders must always be sent by the contracting authority.
Design offices that are involved in the management should not fulfil this role.
The contracting authority must ensure that all candidates have received the same information during this stage of the procurement procedure.
In the event of confusion in the procurement documents or where further questions arise, only the contracting authority may supply information to tenderers.
The design offices responsible for drawing up the specifications may not provide any information.
Throughout this phase, any contact between the design office and the tenderers must be avoided.
They must remain anonymous throughout, as allowing contact with them may adversely affect competition.
Tenders sent by post or submitted directly must be submitted in a sealed envelope.
Tenders submitted electronically must be encrypted.
Different provisions
In Germany, the contracting authority may instruct insured external service suppliers to print and distribute documentation, and their employees must be bound to observe confidentiality.
In Germany, tenders received must be marked with the date of receipt and handed directly and immediately in their original state (sealed) to the official responsible for their safekeeping.
This worker must have no involvement in the procurement procedure.
Tenders received must be locked until the date of opening of the envelopes.
In the Czech Republic, the tenders received are marked with a serial number.
The contracting authority shall issue a report with details of the competition, including the tender number and information on the preparation and receipt of the tender.
There are no specific rules on the safekeeping of tenders.
Key control findings
BRHs
The BRH audited the assignment of the administrative building.
Sending procurement documents
The audited building administration contracting authorities distributed the tender documentation either directly or commissioned an external company to print and distribute it.
The self-employed did not send any documentation.
Information to tenderers
For the questioning and information part of the tender documents, the contracting authority did not follow the separate planning rule from the procurement procedure.
Contrary to the relevant regulations, he often did not mention the names of his liaison officers in the documentation, but rather those of the project management unit.
Some tender notices even included the names of firms or self-employed persons involved in the preparation of the tender documents.
In rare cases, candidates who raised questions during the procurement procedure did not receive identical information.
Marking of quotations
For contracting entities, all tenders received were stamped and acknowledged receipt.
However, in some cases in the negotiated procedure, tenders were accepted by self-employed professionals, in breach of the one-building rule on the prevention of corruption.
Custody of the tenders
Once marked, not all tenders were passed on directly and without delay to the official assigned to keep them in custody locked the tenders in a steel cabinet.
For one contracting entity, tenders were temporarily placed in an open mailbox to which all employees had access.
Another contracting authority placed the box in the corridor without constant supervision and part of the bids thrown into the box stayed there overnight.
The opening of the box was wide and unsecured, making it accessible not only to employees.
SAO
The SAO did not find any shortcomings in this area.
Summary
In both countries, rules are laid down to prevent contacts, in particular between interested companies and design offices cooperating in the procurement process, throughout the process.
Equal treatment is ensured by sending the same information to all candidates.
Rules to prevent manipulation and corruption are appropriate for the receipt, safekeeping and confidentiality of tenders.
In Germany, the relevant requirements are somewhat more developed than in the Czech Republic.
In Germany, the control records collected showed infringements in certain areas.
These irregularities can easily be corrected.
Opening envelopes
Opening envelopes
During the opening of the envelopes, tenders which have been closed so far are opened, their contents are read and published.
The period during which the tenderer is bound by his tender shall begin when the deadline for the submission or opening of the envelopes expires and shall end on the date specified by the contracting authority.
Requirements of European law and prevention of corruption
European legislation does not contain any specific provisions on opening envelopes.
However, national laws and regulations contain extensive requirements to protect both bids and competition during this sensitive phase of the entire procurement process.
The principles of "more eyes," transparency and credibility of the opening process are intended to prevent the manipulation and modification of tenders already submitted.
Provisions of German and Czech procurement law
Identical or similar provisions
The opening of envelopes must be carried out by several persons appointed by the contracting authority.
Compliance with the "more eyes" principle is intended to prevent individuals from secretly manipulating bids.
Candidates may be present when opening the envelopes.
When opening, all envelopes must be placed in front of the Chairman of the Commission and are opened one by one.
Tenders received late shall not be considered.
In order to ensure transparency when opening envelopes, this stage, including the conclusions (results), must be recorded in writing. The record must include, inter alia, the names of the tenderers and the prizes offered.
Minutes must be read and signed by the group leader.
The minutes may also be signed by the tenderers present.
Different provisions
In the Czech Republic, a commission is established for opening envelopes.
The participation of independent experts shall be permitted.
Members of the Commission may also be entrusted with deliberations in subsequent stages of the procedure, i.e. evaluation of tenders.
In Germany, the opening of envelopes takes place in the presence of the manager and the registrar.
The manager must be an employee of the contracting entity.
The manager and, in the case of building construction, the registrar must not be involved in the preparatory phase (creation of tender documents), must not decide on the award of the contract and must not be a member in the subsequent processing (management) of the contract.
The German rules require that the minutes of the opening of the envelopes contain not only the bid prices but also all information on discounts and special (unusual) symptoms, e.g. damaged envelopes on receipt or late tenders.
In the Czech Republic, in addition to the tender prices, the minutes must also contain an indication of the tenderer's language and signature authority.
German regulations require all parts of opened envelopes to be marked to prevent subsequent changes.
Tenders, usually marked with a perforation stating the date, must be kept confidential (secret) after the opening of the envelopes and may only be made available to persons who further process them.
Similar measures do not exist in the Czech Republic.
If a single tender is received in the Czech Republic, the envelope must not be opened and the tenderer must be notified without delay.
In Germany, they do not have this arrangement and, if a single tender is accepted, it is opened and evaluated.
Key control findings
BRHs
Principle of cross-checking
During the opening of the envelopes, the contracting authorities followed the four-eyes principle.
There was always a supervisor and a recorder who was not assigned another role.
However, in rare cases, for small negotiated procedures, natural persons have opened tenders sent to them without the participation of the contracting authority.
Envelope opening progress
Envelope-opening notes did not always document progress.
In some cases, tenders submitted late were not identified and in some cases signatures were missing.
Marking of incoming bids
The contracting authorities marked the perforations with a date, which was in line with the requirements.
Some of them had mislabeled bids in the past, but changed their practice after being warned by the BRH.
THISsegmentISintentionallyLEFTblank
SAO
Time limit for receipt of tenders
In the case of road repairs, for one action, the tenderer's tender was transmitted to the contracting authority with a delay of one month and was nevertheless accepted by the contracting authority.
Summary
Substantial parts of the provisions for opening envelopes are identical in both countries with regard to the principle of "more eyes" and the principle of transparency, and thus make it possible to substantially reduce the risk of corruption and manipulation.
The German rules are more complex compared to the Czech Republic, especially the requirements for precise documentation of the procedure.
The marking, secure storage and respect for the confidentiality of tenders substantially limit the possibility of subsequent amendments to the tenders submitted.
In addition, the staff responsible for opening the envelopes may not carry out any other activities connected with the contract to be awarded.
The findings, despite the discovery of some irregularities, confirm that the rules on opening envelopes are largely applied.
Examination and evaluation of tenders
The contracting authority must examine and evaluate the tenders received within the time limit set.
It must also award the contract to the tenderer which best meets the award criteria in the tender.
Requirements of European law and prevention of corruption
EU law sets out the process of assessing and evaluating bids in several steps:
The first step is to check the suitability of candidates.
They must be in a good economic and financial position and have the requisite professional and technical knowledge and skills.
Candidates who have ever been finally convicted of bribery or fraud must always be excluded from procurement procedures.
Tenders from candidates who have not been eliminated must be evaluated for numerical accuracy, technical suitability and value for money on the basis of previously published award criteria.
The contracting authority must identify the most economically advantageous tender and in particular examine all significantly low price offers.
Review and evaluation is particularly sensitive and prone to manipulation.
For example, tenderers may be excluded without a legitimate reason, or tenders that do not meet the requirements may be retained in competition.
The outcome of the evaluation may be improperly influenced by the preference or disadvantage of a tenderer, thereby preventing the selection of the most economically advantageous tender.
The contracting authority must therefore take special precautions when selecting the persons involved in the process and must ensure that they meet the requirements of good repute and technical knowledge.
They also need to make sure that the whole process and the decisions taken are transparent and credible.
Provisions of German and Czech procurement law
Identical or similar provisions
In line with EU requirements, the relevant provisions in both countries require the examination and evaluation of bids in stages.
The requirements for demonstrating the suitability of tenderers, the evaluation criteria and their weightings set out in the notice or in the tender dossier shall not be altered during the evaluation.
The criteria laid down for assessing the suitability of candidates may no longer be applied.
A further evaluation may only be carried out on the basis of the evaluation criteria.
The tenders submitted must first be examined from a formal point of view to ensure that they are complete or do not contain suspicious elements which might encourage manipulation.
If tenderers are excluded from further examination and evaluation of the tenders submitted by them, they must be informed immediately.
Where the evaluation of tenders results in the finding of an unreasonably low tender price, the tenderer concerned must be given the opportunity to comment on the situation before any elimination of its tender.
The reasons for the decision shall be transparently documented.
Different provisions
In Germany, tenderers must observe confidentiality and only authorised persons may participate in the evaluation of tenders.
Only the contracting authority may examine the mathematical accuracy of tenders.
Only staff who are not involved in the decision-making phase of the selection of the contractor or in the implementation phase of the project may be entrusted with the evaluation.
There are no precise provisions on who can carry out further reviews and evaluations.
Where external companies are entrusted with the examination and evaluation of tenders, they must be aware that tenders are confidential and respect this fact honestly.
They may only make proposals for a decision to the contracting authority.
In the Czech Republic, tenders must be examined and evaluated by a committee appointed by the contracting authority, which draws up a report proposing the conclusion of the contract.
However, in both countries the final decision is up to the contracting authority.
If, in Germany, the documents and declarations required in connection with the submission of tenders are incomplete, the tenderers concerned must subsequently submit the required documents within six days.
THISsegmentISintentionallyLEFTblank
If tenderers fail to comply with this obligation, their tenders must be eliminated.
In the Czech Republic, the contracting authority may decide whether to give the tenderer the opportunity to fill in the missing documents.
If the tenderer fails to prove them, he must be expelled.
In Germany, it is necessary to examine not only the total price but also the unit prices.
Where the price structure of the tender shows that prices for parts of the overall project have been estimated excessively high or low, tenderers must be asked to explain.
If they do not provide explanations and if the contracting authority can demonstrate that the tender contains countervailing prices, such tenders must be excluded.
This is to prevent bidders from submitting price calculations on the assumption that substantial quantitative changes will be made to the different parts of the overall project, which may lead to a significant increase in project costs.
In the Czech Republic, the total price of a work is evaluated and the prices of individual parts are not examined in detail.
Valuation compensation is not a reason to exclude a bid.
In Germany, a contract cannot be awarded to a tenderer who offers a disproportionately high or low price.
Where one or more tenders differ in price by 10% or more from the price estimated by the contracting authority or from the other tenders, tenderers must demonstrate that their tender has been credibly and convincingly calculated.
Where they are unable to do so, the sponsor should exclude them.
In the Czech Republic, a procurement procedure may be cancelled after a price review if only tenders with unreasonably low prices have been received and the tenderers have not substantiated those prices.
If the price of the tender is substantially higher than the contracting authority's estimate, that does not justify a refusal to award the contract to the tenderer concerned (unlike German procurement law).
If conduct intended to restrict competition is suspected in Germany, the contracting authority is obliged to notify the competent authority immediately.
The latter must decide how to proceed and whether it is appropriate to inform the antitrust authority or the law enforcement authorities.
They must also approve (e.g. if the contract exceeds €50,000 and the contract has not been awarded to the lowest bidder) whether the contract will be awarded when the contract is negotiated or whether the procurement procedure will be cancelled.
Czech rules do not regulate this area.
Key control findings
BRHs
Examination of suitability and award criteria
Most of the procurement procedures examined were in line with the relevant requirements.
In the case of some self-employed recommendations from potential suppliers, the contracting authorities themselves did not check the suitability of the tenderers.
THISsegmentISintentionallyLEFTblank
In some cases, insufficient documentation was provided on whether the contracting entity had taken into account all the published evaluation criteria when making the choice or whether the selection decision was based solely on the price criterion.
Selection of interested parties and confidentiality of tenders
Some contracting entities used the services of unauthorised persons, such as self-employed persons or employees of building authorities.
They were integrated into the planning or implementation process of construction projects, performing functions that were to be performed exclusively by the contracting authority.
The contracting authorities tried to justify this fact by their insufficient human resources in the sector.
In some cases, they used self-employed professionals to examine the offers of services of persons who were not formally bound to respect the confidentiality of tenders and to perform their obligations under the contract with due diligence.
As regards contracting authorities, there was generally no conclusive evidence as to whether tenders in the custody of self-employed professionals were available only to designated persons.
Examination and mathematical control of tenders
Some contracting entities handed over open envelopes for further examination to self-employed professionals without first carrying out the prescribed examination of tenders and mathematical examination and without documenting the results of those examinations.
In the event of any intention to manipulate, third parties could make changes to tenders which could not be identified by the contracting authority.
Examination of the documents revealed a number of cases where unusual elements in the tenders were not adequately identified or where information on prices and products was missing.
Performance audits
A sample check by one contracting authority revealed that in 75% of procurement procedures, the most advantageous tenders deviated by more than 10% from the pre-estimated costs.
One in 20 procurement procedures was cancelled due to inadequate tender prices.
In many cases, there was a lack of evidence or sufficient documentation of the screening of tenders or of discussions with tenderers for further clarification.
In many cases, detailed information was also missing, e.g. on the unit prices of the most important invoiced items.
Changes in market prices or increases in material costs were often cited as reasons for the deviations.
The contracting entities argued that it was difficult to identify and demonstrate countervailing prices.
In the case of one call for tenders, made by an entity outside the circle of ordinary contracting authorities, a 66% lower price was offered compared to the initial cost estimate.
Data on closer examination of cost or identification of the reasons for deviations were not recorded.
In clarifying this issue, the contracting authority found no indication of price dumping by the tenderer to which the contract might have been awarded.
It was therefore not clarified whether the original cost estimate was inflated or whether the price offered was too low.
Subsequently, the contracting authority rated the tenderer as a "low-cost company" which was unable to meet either the qualitative requirements or the deadlines.
In another procurement procedure, the contracting authority awarded the contract to a tenderer whose price was 35% higher than the initial cost estimate.
The contracting authority did not cancel the tendering procedure, arguing that the tenderer could be presumed not to have based its pricing on unreasonably high costs.
Suspected irregularities
Two cases of irregularities were found in one contracting entity and they were reported to the firm responsible for technical supervision.
In one call for tenders, two tenderers submitted tenders containing identical price calculations, which prompted the contracting authority to exclude both tenders.
The checks did not reveal any substantial deficiencies in the application of other provisions.
SAO
Verification of suitability
For many construction projects, such as university buildings, roads and railways, contracting entities did not exclude tenderers from participating in the procurement procedure even though they did not meet the requirements of the contracting authority specified in the specifications, or did not substantiate qualifications to the required extent, or, conversely, one contracting authority failed to substantiate or explain the reason for the exclusion of a candidate who met the requirements.
Rating
In the case of the construction of a waste water treatment plant, the various evaluation criteria of the tendering procedure were imposed on the contractor, but the contracting authority did not comply with them during the procedure.
For the rail infrastructure development action, the weightings of the evaluation criteria were not set at the start of the procedure when the contract was awarded, but only at the beginning of the evaluation.
For the road repair project, the evaluation of the criterion "reduced construction time" was not carried out correctly.
For the second and third place bidders, the evaluation incorrectly indicated a greater reduction in construction time than indicated in their bids.
Documentation of reasons for candidate selection
In the case of three actions connected with housing support, the contracting authority did not demonstrate on the basis of which criteria it assessed the accepted bids and how it selected the most suitable of them.
Correct selection of suppliers of small-scale contracts connected with the construction of a ring road with an estimated price of up to 500 thousand CZK
It was not possible to verify the CZK, as the suppliers were selected only on the basis of telephone consultations on the price and date of performance, but the documents of these negotiations were not prepared.
Summary
In both countries, the rules for the verification and evaluation of tenders comply with EU requirements.
However, they differ with regard to the specific steps of the procedure and the choice of the individuals involved.
Unlike in the Czech Republic, these steps of the procurement process in Germany are governed by stricter and more detailed requirements, especially as regards the application of the cross-principle and the protection of tenders.
The same applies to the rules for verifying the fairness of the prices offered.
In both countries, in some cases of inspections, it was found that substantial steps in the process of verifying and evaluating tenders were not documented at all and, if so, only partially.
The absence of control tools and transparency in decision-making during the examination and evaluation of tenders allows for manipulation.
The inspections showed serious defects, such as the transmission of tenders to third parties without prior appropriate screening and documentation of tenders by the contracting authority.
Where contracting entities entrust a third party with the performance of functions related to the examination and evaluation of tenders, appropriate measures and increased monitoring of measures against the increased possibility of manipulation are necessary.
In checking the legitimacy of the prices, the audited German contracting authorities did not in all cases use the full set of tools available and did not look at their own price structure.
In the Czech Republic, weaknesses were found in the evaluation of the suitability of tenders and the use of evaluation criteria.
Completion of the procurement procedure
Following the examination and evaluation of tenders, the award process shall be concluded by the award of the contract or the cancellation of the procedure.
Requirements of European law and prevention of corruption
In the case of a European procedure, all tenderers must be informed in advance of the award decision.
Suppliers shall have the opportunity to review the decision taken within a limited period of 10 or 15 days and to request its review where appropriate.
THISsegmentISintentionallyLEFTblank
The deadline of 10 days applies to electronic submissions and 15 days to other submissions.
The contract may not be concluded before the expiry of that period or before the outcome of the review procedure.
If the review body rejects the complaint and upholds the decision, candidates and tenderers must be given the opportunity to have the correctness of the application of the procurement right reviewed by an independent tribunal.
This measure ensures that no contracting entity will be able to circumvent the legal protection of suppliers by implementing procurement decisions quickly.
The contracting entity shall adequately document the progress of the procedure.
Provisions of German and Czech procurement law
Identical or similar provisions
Both countries implemented the management information obligations.
In both countries, the final decision on the outcome of the procurement procedure rests with the contracting authority.
However, it cannot arbitrarily depart from the results of a properly carried out verification and evaluation.
The contracting authority is to conclude the contract within a specified period.
This is due to the fact that, after this period, the tenderer is not bound by its tender.
The content of the contract must not deviate from the winning bid.
Unsuccessful tenderers shall be informed of the outcome in good time, stating the reason why their tender has not been accepted.
Cancellation of the procurement procedure is admissible only if certain conditions are met, e.g. if the prices in the tenders do not correspond to the specifications.
Different provisions
In the Czech Republic, the mandatory waiting period before the conclusion of a contract of 10 or 15 days applies to both European and national procedures.
In Germany, the equivalent provision applies only to European proceedings.
In all other procedures in Germany, tenderers cannot be informed of the award of the contract before a decision is taken on its EU provision and review award.
In the Czech Republic, a procurement procedure must be cancelled if a single tender has been received or only one tender meets the requirements for its consideration and evaluation.
In Germany, the contract must be awarded even if a single tender has been received, provided that the tender meets the conditions laid down in the tender dossier and is cost-effective.
Key control findings
BRHs
The audits found few irregularities in the completion of the audited procurement processes.
In some cases of European proceedings, companies received empowerment letters before the notification period expired, which may invalidate the contract.
Also in several cases of open procedures, the empowerment letters contained modifications incompatible with the content of the tender, e.g. new deadlines.
In some cases, contracting entities did not meet the time limits for the award of contracts.
In one open procedure, the contracting authority selected the second most advantageous tender because a bidder offering a price more than 50% lower no longer wished to fulfil the contract at the price originally offered.
SAO
During the reconstruction and construction of the university, in one case the contracting authority did not conclude a contract with the contractor for work in accordance with the draft contract contained in the bid of the selected tenderer.
For some of the road repair and maintenance actions, the construction of the motorway and the ring road, the contracts were concluded later than provided for by law.
In the case of building modifications in former military retreats, the contracting authority sent a notice of the selection of the most suitable tender to all tenderers only after the statutory deadline.
Summary
Both countries have implemented EU rules on the closure of procurement procedures.
Legal protection of unsuccessful candidates contributes to reducing corruption.
The Czech Public Procurement Act includes the institute of legal protection even in cases of contracts below EU limits.
In both countries, it was found that the limited time limit for contracting was exceeded.
This may cause adverse economic consequences and increase the risk of manipulation.
In future, contracting entities should eliminate such shortcomings when concluding a procurement procedure.
Documentation, notifications, information obligations
The procurement process, with all its different stages, relevant findings and decisions, including the relevant justifications, shall be documented in a transparent manner.
The completion of the procurement procedure at European level must be published.
Data on procurement procedures are summarised in EU statistics.
Requirements of European law and prevention of corruption
The obligation to document procurement procedures is in line with the fundamental requirement of the EU-wide principle of transparency.
Only proper and time-relevant documentation on decision-making and justification guarantees transparency in the procurement process and, above all, guarantees effective legal protection of the interests of each tenderer in cases where the value of the contract exceeds European thresholds.
Appropriate documentation is also an essential part of anti-corruption measures, as the obligation to provide detailed reasons for individual decisions and their merits significantly reduces the scope for manipulation.
In the case of European procurement procedures, the contracting authority must notify the EU Publications Office within 48 days of the date of award of the contract.
The Office publishes the notification in the Official Journal of the EU and this fulfils the requirement of transparency.
The contracting authority must summarise the essential details of its procurement procedures and communicate them to the European Commission via the relevant national ministries.
On this basis, the European Commission publishes annual statistics to provide an overview of the development of the single market in the construction sector.
Provisions of German and Czech procurement law
Identical or similar provisions
Both countries have fully transposed the provisions of the European Act on Documentation and Notification Obligations into their national legislation.
The provisions on documentation and notification for European statistics are almost identical in both countries.
Different provisions
In the Czech Republic, the obligation to notify the award of a contract applies not only to European procedures but also to national procurement procedures.
The notice period is only 15 days from the conclusion of the contract.
In Germany, the obligation to notify procurement procedures, in addition to those mandatory under European law, applies only to restricted procedures exceeding the limit of €25,000 (CZK 625,000) and direct procedures exceeding the limit of €15,000 (CZK 375,000) including VAT.
Only the parties are informed below that limit.
Key control findings
BRHs
Documentation
A large number of entries have not been fully documented.
The notification did not contain all the necessary information, such as the reasons for the delivery specifications and national rules.
In the case of one procedure, the contracting authority did not explain why it had concluded a contract with a tenderer whose tender did not contain the mandatory requirements.
In several cases, some of the required records were also missing.
Publication of the award of the contract
In some European procedures, the contracting authority erred in publishing a notice.
In the case of several awards, evidence of publication of the notice was lacking.
Notification obligation
The statistically examined files included the required minimum of information and were produced and sent in a timely manner.
Prior transparency
In most cases, the contracting authority published information on the outcome of the contract.
In the case of one contracting authority, the contract information was not disclosed due to a communication error.
It took the sponsor six months to spot the error.
Consequently, the outcome of the proceedings was not published during that period.
SAO
Documentation
For events related to the organisation of a major sporting event, significant shortcomings were found in the documentation on the conduct of the procurement procedure, such as incorrect information on bid prices in the minutes of the evaluation committee meetings.
Moreover, some of the documents opening the envelopes were not signed.
Publication of the award of the contract
In the preparation of repairs and maintenance of roads, motorway construction and ring roads, public procurement notices were sent after the deadline or were not published.
Summary
The provisions on documentation and notification and notification obligations include European requirements and are almost identical in both countries.
This generally ensures the transparency of procurement and final selection procedures.
Czech reporting obligations are somewhat stricter compared to German ones.
The audits revealed weaknesses substantially reducing the value of documentation as a means of preventing corruption.
THISsegmentISintentionallyLEFTblank
Contracting entities should continue to strengthen the transparency of their procurement procedures and fully comply with the notification and notification requirements.
Summary evaluation and recommendations
Parallel audits of the SAO and the BRH were focused on two areas that are essential for the public procurement process in both the Czech Republic and Germany, namely the application of EU procurement law and the prevention of corruption.
EU legislation provides an opportunity for international competition within the EU with a view to creating a single European market.
Prevention measures aim not only to avoid or at least reduce corruption, but also to prevent financial damage and possible losses of confidence in the integrity of government.
The audit findings of both audit institutions - SAO and BRH - with regard to the application of the procurement law of both countries and the comparison of its relevant provisions give an internal view of common and different elements that can help both countries in further development and application of this law.
Application of EU procurement law
Both countries have transposed EU public procurement law into their national legislation.
There is thus an important common basis in both countries for public procurement and the fulfilment of basic requirements in the areas of: competition; equal treatment of candidates; transparency of procurement procedures.
Contracting entities reinforce these requirements partly through very far-reaching measures.
The Czech Republic has extended the provisions on public procurement requiring a European procedure to procedures below EU thresholds and is therefore governed by national legislation.
However, inspections have shown that contracting entities do not always fully comply with all relevant provisions of EU requirements.
German contracting authorities should continue to improve the quality of their procurement procedures in order to eliminate these inconsistencies, taking into account the implementation of EU requirements.
In the Czech Republic, the audits identified risks affecting economy and achieving value for money.
These include, in particular, the incomplete definition of the subject-matter of the public contract, which resulted in additional works, which were often awarded in breach of the law, the insufficient definition of the expected price, and the required qualifications, which narrow down the possible range of tenderers and thus reduce competition, particularly in the field of transport construction.
Prevention of corruption
Joint inspections did not aim to detect cases of corruption.
The reason was that the BRH and the SAO do not have sufficient tools for this.
However, they may examine whether the competent authorities of both countries take measures to reduce corruption and manipulation in public procurement and how these measures are applied.
As regards the prevention of corruption, the two countries focused on different aspects.
In particular, the German provisions allow for broad and unrestricted competition, transparency and controls as a central element in the prevention of corruption.
The Czech regulations aim primarily at strengthening the transparency of procurement procedures.
BRHs
In Germany, there are general principles on the prevention of corruption that apply to the entire federal administration.
These principles have also been included in public procurement requirements and have been adopted to meet the specific needs of such procurement.
The relevant provisions specify, sometimes very precisely, ways of complying with the requirements to prevent corruption during the procurement procedure and to select the persons included in it.
In the BRH's view, the principle of preference for open procurement procedures and calls for participation is a particularly appropriate tool to substantially reduce the risk of corruption in public procurement, while restricting competition increases the risk of manipulation.
Risk analysis and adequate controls (in particular the cross-checking principle), transparency requirements, procurement documentation and various organisational measures are also effective mechanisms for preventing corruption and manipulation.
The BRH therefore sees no need for substantial changes to the current provisions that public institutions must comply with when awarding public contracts.
Anti-corruption measures are widely applied in practice.
Irregularities found in some areas have highlighted weaknesses in compliance with certain requirements.
Therefore, contracting entities should provide further training to their employees in order to make them aware of anti-corruption requirements and to further raise their awareness in this area.
A significant part of the weaknesses revealed relates to activities delegated by the construction administration to private entrepreneurs.
However, the absence of adequate controls on the delegation of powers increases the risk of abuse.
Activities susceptible to manipulation and corruption should therefore only be transferred to third parties if the administration can guarantee a sufficient degree of protection against unauthorised interference.
SAO
In the Czech Republic, anti-corruption strategies have been adopted at government level, which also significantly affect the area of public procurement.
The SAO audits took place in the period when one of the most important parts of the strategy was implemented, namely a significant change in the Public Procurement Act and the adoption of related implementing regulations.
The strategy adopted and the new legislation can be effective steps to improve the situation.
According to the new regulations, the contracting authority must, for example, justify the effectiveness of the public contract, the determination of the expected value of the public contract, the adequacy of the requirements for qualification prerequisites, the establishment of the basic evaluation criteria and the method of evaluating tenders, and duly document and publish everything.
Recommendations
Overall, the SAO and the BRH have a favourable opinion of the ways of implementing EU requirements.
Their wide application in practice has confirmed that the EU's requirements for competition, equal treatment and transparency constitute an appropriate basis for public procurement within the EU.
The transnational model, established in the EU, can also serve as a basis for similar projects aimed at facilitating and promoting international competition.
Taking into account the great potential for material and non-material damage incurred as a result of manipulation and corruption in connection with public procurement, the BRH and the SAO insist that it is the responsibility of contracting authorities to continue measures against corruption and manipulation and to improve them where possible.
In particular, organisational structures and procurement processes should be continuously monitored for weaknesses and risks in order to identify and respond appropriately to the need for corrective action.
Compared to the practice in Germany, it has emerged that the proportion of open procurement procedures in the Czech Republic, which give room to competition and substantially reduce the possibility of corrupt behaviour, should increase.
Procurement in instalments may also have a positive impact on the price obtained, where this is permissible and appropriate for the contracting entity. A higher number of bidders puts pressure on the price achieved.
The organisation of the procurement procedure also has an impact on its progress.
In Germany, it is provided by special state organisations, while in the Czech Republic, with the exception of transport constructions, it is organised by the central authorities themselves or through contractual organisations.
Both the SAO and the BRH wish to further develop knowledge in their work where appropriate and to address it with their parliament and government beyond their joint project.
Moreover, they are convinced that the principles of procurement law and their implementation, as described in this report, will also be of interest at the level of the International Organisation of Supreme Audit Institutions (INTOSAI), as these principles and findings are of general importance for the effective fight against corruption and for guaranteeing that public contracts are awarded under a regime of well-functioning and honest competition and that value for money is achieved.
